Starting: *Arbitrate and recommend* (6/6)

Now I have full analysis from both sides. Here's my arbitration:

---

## PR Numbering Explanation

You're not wrong about the 40s. **There are two repos with separate PR counters:**

| Repo | Role | Latest PR # |
|---|---|---|
| **Coldaine/ALAS** (fork) | Your working fork | PRs up to **#48** |
| **Zuosizhu/Alas-with-Dashboard** (origin) | Upstream target | PRs up to **#29** |

The `pr-44` through `pr-47` branches were PRs **on your fork** (Coldaine/ALAS), not on origin. When you open a cross-fork PR *to* Zuosizhu, it gets a Zuosizhu number. So:

- **Fork PR #43** = `feature/adb-vision-clean` = **Origin PR #29** (same branch, same content)
- **Fork PR #44-47** = the 4 experiment branches (all closed on fork, never opened on origin)
- **Fork PR #38** = `feature/async-adb-decoupling` (merged on fork = merged on origin too)

So yes — `feature/adb-vision-clean` as PR #29 on origin **does** incorporate the synthesis of fork PRs #44-47.

---

## Arbitration: PR #25 (chore/upstream-sync-alas-wrapped)

**Recommendation: CLOSE IT. Cherry-pick the black-frame benchmark fix.**

The "keep" case correctly identifies the black-frame benchmark fix as genuinely valuable. But the "close" case wins on every other dimension:

1. **Scope creep is real** — 11 commits mixing 5 concerns (admin service, upstream sync, benchmark fix, Akashi preservation, config) is exactly the pattern that caused PR #16's regression. The fix is to decompose, not to rebase the whole thing.
2. **Admin service is architecturally premature** — it belongs in Phase II recovery-agent work, which hasn't been designed yet. Merging half of it now creates orphaned code.
3. **Upstream sync should be done fresh** — 6 weeks of drift means a fresh sync following `UPSTREAM_SYNC.md` will be cleaner than rebasing a stale 11-commit branch.

**Action plan:**
- Close PR #25 on both repos
- Cherry-pick the black-frame benchmark fix into a focused 1-commit PR
- Schedule a fresh upstream sync as a separate task
- Delete the `fork/chore/upstream-sync-alas-wrapped` remote branch

---

## Arbitration: PR #29 (feature/adb-vision-clean)

**Recommendation: KEEP IT, but clean it up before merging.**

The "close" case makes fair points about stub backends and scope mixing, but overstates the prematurity argument:

1. **It's the right infrastructure at the right time.** The entire project vision requires a clean ADB tool layer decoupled from ALAS internals. adb_vision achieves exactly this — zero ALAS imports, async, pluggable backends. Waiting for a "formal Phase II design" to build the most basic tool layer is waterfall thinking.
2. **The stub backends are honest, not broken.** DroidCast/scrcpy raising `NotImplementedError` with tracking issues is better than pretending they work. `screencap` works on most devices; MEmu-specific backends can land incrementally.
3. **Scope mixing is fixable.** The config/docs commits (3-5) should be split out, but that's an interactive rebase, not a reason to close.
4. **Tool duplication is intentional.** adb_vision is the successor to `alas_mcp_server`'s ADB tools. The migration path is: ship adb_vision, migrate callers, remove duplicates from `alas_mcp_server`. Both existing simultaneously during transition is normal.

**Action plan:**
- Interactive rebase to drop/squash the config noise commits
- Keep the `feat: create adb_vision/` core commit + docs commit
- Merge once cleaned up — it's the active working branch with real value

---

## Summary

| PR | Verdict | Reason |
|---|---|---|
| **#25** (upstream-sync) | **CLOSE** | Scope creep, stale, admin service premature. Cherry-pick benchmark fix only. |
| **#29** (adb-vision-clean) | **KEEP + CLEAN** | Right infrastructure, right time. Drop config noise commits, merge. |

Want me to execute either of these actions?

## codex: Where did I see where did you get cute? The subage...

Where did I see where did you get cute? The subagents attach to this X, you till done. * **Subagent & Execution Strategy**
* Spawn parallel subagents whenever possible for reading, analyzing, and implementing.
* Implement tasks all the way through using subagents and retry mechanisms.
* Think processes through from beginning to end before executing.


* **Planning & Documentation**
* Read any and all relevant documentation (including newer forks) to gather previously discussed plans.
* Combine found information with current context and write the *entire* plan to a Markdown document *before* taking action.
* Constantly update the to-do list and agent memories.
* Add the master plan to the to-do list to establish a clear execution loop (e.g., checking what's next, running navigation tools).


* **Clarification & Communication**
* Use the "ask questions" tool to thoroughly interview the user and ensure absolute clarity on all key points before proceeding.


* **Repository & Branch Management**
* Perform deep purges of local and remote branches, aggressively pruning anything over a month old.
* Survey recent work to determine what is furthest along.
* Check off work carefully to ensure no current repository data is lost during the pruning process.


* **Pull Request Reviews**
* For open PRs, spawn a subagent to act as a "devil's advocate" to weigh pros and cons.
* Arbitrate between the arguments and provide a clear, reasoned recommendation.


* **Architecture & Environment**
* Anchor on MIMU (launch it with admin permissions via multi-manager; high memory usage is acceptable).
* Ensure new implementations stand on their own while still utilizing the existing state engine (powered by OCR and lightweight vision matching/small vision LLM).


* **Tool Creation & Manual Piloting**
* When a tool is missing to get from point A to B, manually pilot the process.
* Take screenshots and record actions during manual piloting.
* Assess the success of the actions, log the results, and create a blueprint to turn the manual process into an automated tool later.


* **Error Handling & Fallbacks**
* If an error occurs, manually grab screenshots and attempt to visually diagnose the issue.
* If all troubleshooting fails, restart the emulator to recover the tooling state.

## codex: Here's additional color. Make sure you're followin...

Here's additional color. Make sure you're following this. ster Plan & Permanent Operating Loop (exactly as you described)

Master Scheduler
Runs continuously.
Holds a list of timed tasks (“every 2 hours: collect commissions”, “in 47 min: pick up exercise rewards”, “daily at 06:00: full dailies”, etc.).
Tracks deadlines, refresh timers, and priorities from PatrickCustom.json + any ad-hoc entries.
Core Agent Loop (identical in dev mode and autonomous mode, forever)
Ask scheduler: “What is next? Is anything urgent or due now?”
If a matching deterministic tool exists → run it immediately (e.g. goto_main_menu(), collect_commissions(priority=...), run_exercise()).
Tool succeeds → update persistent state (oil/gem/coin, current page, task queue) → loop.
No tool exists / tool fails / unexpected screen → drop to manual piloting mode (see below).

Deterministic Tool Layer (hot path, preferred)
Powered by lightweight behind-the-scenes state machine + OCR + small vision matching (template-style, fast, no reasoning LLM).
These are first-class tools the agent always tries first.
Manual Piloting / Vision Mode (always available fallback, permanent)
Triggered whenever deterministic layer cannot proceed.
Sequence:
Take screenshot
Reasoning VLM (Gemini Flash) analyzes screen + history + goal
Issue raw adb_tap / adb_swipe or chain of raw actions to get from A to B
On success: record exact sequence, outcomes, and create blueprint (“this is how we turn A→B into a new deterministic tool”)
Log for later codification (human or agent turns blueprint into permanent tool)

Error Handling (at any time, any driver)
“Uh oh” detected → immediately:
Grab screenshot(s)
VLM analyzes what went wrong
Attempt recovery with raw taps or new actions
If unrecoverable → ultimate fallback: restart MEmu via admin plug-in (returns to clean known state)

Persistent State (always exposed)
Oil, gems, coins, action points, current page, scheduler queue, recent history — queryable by any agent at any time.
Single Harness Rule
Same MCP tools (deterministic + screenshot + raw touch + vision call) used by:
Coding agent while building today
Standalone Gemini agent running forever tomorrow


This loop is the permanent state of the system.
Deterministic tools grow over time, but vision + raw control + scheduler + error restart path are always present and always used exactly as described.
That is the full, final master plan in one place.

The Permanent Vision (the loop that runs forever)

Master scheduler continuously checks: “What’s next? Anything urgent or due?”
Agent always tries deterministic tool first (state machine + OCR + lightweight vision matching behind it).
Tool succeeds → update state → continue.
No tool or failure → vision/manual piloting mode (screenshot + reasoning VLM + raw taps/swipes).
On success in manual mode → record what worked + create blueprint for new deterministic tool.
Error at any time → screenshot + VLM analysis → recovery attempt → ultimate fallback (restart MEmu via admin plug-in).
Persistent state (resources, page, queue, timers) always exposed.
Same harness and loop for coding agent today and standalone Gemini agent forever.

How We Get There (the exact path, using everything you said)
Here is the development process you described, step by step:

Start with the master scheduler tracking timed tasks (“every couple of hours do X”, “in 2 hours pick this up”, refresh timers, etc.).
Coding agent runs the core loop manually (same loop the final agent will use).
For every next action:
First try any existing deterministic tool (e.g. “go to main menu”, “collect commissions”).
If tool exists and works → success, move on.
If no tool exists yet or it fails → drop to manual piloting:
Take screenshot(s)
Use reasoning vision (VLM) to understand the screen and goal
Manually pilot with raw touch (taps/swipes) to go from A to B
When successful: note what happened, record the exact sequence
Generate a blueprint (“this is exactly how to turn A→B into a new deterministic tool”)


Later (during building or even in autonomous runs) the blueprint becomes a real first-class deterministic tool.
Deterministic tools grow over time → agent uses them more often.
Vision + manual mode stays permanently available (never removed) for anything unknown, errors, or new content.
Error handling is always on: any problem immediately triggers screenshot + VLM + recovery or MEmu restart.
Single harness ensures the coding agent today and the standalone agent tomorrow use the exact same tools and loop.

That is precisely the path you laid out.
Vision is permanent (builder aid + normal fallback).
Manual piloting today becomes more deterministic tools tomorrow.
The loop never changes.

Zero translation cost when we flip to full autonomy

Slide 8: How We Get There (The Path)

Today: Coding agent runs the exact loop manually (heavy piloting + vision)
Every successful manual session → blueprint → new deterministic tool
Over time: Deterministic tools handle more and more

Goal: Nothing can break the bot permanently.
Absolute Requirements (TDD):

Any unexpected state or exception triggers screenshot + VLM diagnosis
Recovery attempts (raw actions) are tried
Final fallback: restart_memu_via_admin_plugin() returns to clean main screen
Full error path has its own live TDD test suite

Success Metric: Deliberately cause 5 different failures → bot recovers or restarts MEmu every time.
Phase 5: Full Autonomous Mode + Polish

Absolute Requirements (TDD):

One command starts autonomous mode using the exact same loop
Logging of every decision, tool call, and blueprint created
Dashboard query tools work (resources, current page, queue)
24-hour stability test (must run without human intervention)

Success Metric: Bot runs overnight completing scheduled tasks using deterministic tools where possible, falling back to vision/manual only when needed.

## codex: [$find-skills](C:\Users\pmacl\.agents\skills\find-...

[$find-skills](C:\Users\pmacl\.agents\skills\find-skills\SKILL.md) ## ️ Polycam Raw Data Structure

From the polyform spec, the export looks like this:

```
capture-folder/
  raw.glb              # reconstructed mesh (binary glTF)
  thumbnail.jpg
  polycam.mp4
  mesh_info.json       # metadata + alignmentTransform
  keyframes/
    images/            # RGB keyframes
    corrected_images/  # post-optimization RGB
    cameras/           # raw ARKit camera JSON (pose + intrinsics)
    corrected_cameras/ # optimized camera JSON
    depth/             # 16-bit PNG depth maps
    confidence/        # confidence maps per frame
```

##  Best 3D File Type for Comparison

**PLY.** It's the lingua franca across your whole toolchain: RTAB-Map exports it, Polycam exports it, Open3D reads it, CloudCompare reads it, Nerfstudio reads it. It carries both geometry and color, works as point cloud or mesh, and it's unambiguous. OBJ is fine for textured meshes but PLY is simpler for programmatic comparison (computing distances, overlap, registration error).

Export Polycam's processed result as **PLY point cloud** for the apples-to-apples comparison.

##  Agent Prompt

---

**Task: Explore and catalog a Polycam raw data export, then assess pipeline readiness for Nerfstudio/DN-Splatter ingestion.**

I have a Polycam LiDAR capture exported with Developer Mode enabled. The raw data export is at `[PATH]`. I need you to do the following:

**Phase 1: Inventory**

1. List the full directory tree of the export. Confirm the presence of: `raw.glb`, `mesh_info.json`, and the `keyframes/` subdirectory containing `images/`, `corrected_images/`, `cameras/`, `corrected_cameras/`, `depth/`, `confidence/`.
2. Count the number of files in each `keyframes/` subdirectory. These counts should match (one camera JSON, one RGB image, one depth map, and one confidence map per keyframe).
3. Report any missing or mismatched counts.

**Phase 2: Inspect the data**

4. Open one camera JSON from `keyframes/cameras/` and one from `keyframes/corrected_cameras/`. Print both. Identify: focal length (fx, fy), principal point (cx, cy), image dimensions, and the 4x4 extrinsic matrix (camera-to-world pose). Note any differences between the raw and corrected versions. The corrected version contains Polycam's globally optimized poses; the raw version contains ARKit's original poses.
5. Open one depth map from `keyframes/depth/`. It should be a 16-bit PNG. Report: image dimensions, min/max pixel values, and the depth scale (how pixel values convert to meters; check the camera JSON for a `depth_scale` or similar field, or check if values are already in millimeters or meters).
6. Open one confidence map from `keyframes/confidence/`. Report: image dimensions, unique pixel values (typically 0, 1, 2 for low/medium/high confidence), and the distribution across those values.
7. Open `mesh_info.json`. Report: number of faces, number of vertices, and the `alignmentTransform` matrix (4x4, column-major). This transform aligns the mesh coordinate system to the camera coordinate system.

**Phase 3: Pipeline compatibility check**

8. Clone `https://github.com/PolyCam/polyform` and read its README and converter code. Confirm whether `polyform` can convert this export to Nerfstudio's expected input format. Run the instant-ngp converter as a test: `python3 -m polyform.convert [PATH] --format ingp` and report what it produces.
9. Check whether Nerfstudio has a native Polycam data parser. Run: `ns-process-data polycam --help` (if Nerfstudio is installed) and report the expected arguments. If Nerfstudio is not installed, search its documentation at `https://docs.nerf.studio` for "polycam" and report what the docs say about input format requirements.
10. Check whether DN-Splatter or lidar-gsplat (Gaussian Splatting methods in the Nerfstudio ecosystem) can accept depth supervision from Polycam's depth maps. Search the DN-Splatter repo (`https://github.com/maturk/dn-splatter`) for "polycam" or "depth" input handling.

**Phase 4: Summary report**

Produce a table:

| Item | Status | Notes |
|---|---|---|
| Keyframe count | ? | |
| Camera intrinsics present | ? | fx, fy, cx, cy values |
| Corrected poses present | ? | Polycam-optimized vs raw ARKit |
| Depth maps present + readable | ? | dimensions, scale, format |
| Confidence maps present | ? | value distribution |
| polyform → ingp conversion | ? | success/failure |
| Nerfstudio polycam parser | ? | available/unavailable |
| DN-Splatter depth input | ? | compatible format? |

Then state clearly: **what is the shortest path from this export to a Gaussian Splatting reconstruction on an RTX 5090?** If there are conversion gaps, describe exactly what script or transformation is needed.

## codex: [$heavy-skill-creator](C:\Users\pmacl\.codex\skill...

[$heavy-skill-creator](C:\Users\pmacl\.codex\skills\heavy-skill-creator\SKILL.md) ---
name: deepinit
description: Deep codebase initialization with hierarchical AGENTS.md documentation
---

# Deep Init Skill

Creates comprehensive, hierarchical AGENTS.md documentation across the entire codebase.

## Core Concept

AGENTS.md files serve as **AI-readable documentation** that helps agents understand:
- What each directory contains
- How components relate to each other
- Special instructions for working in that area
- Dependencies and relationships

## Hierarchical Tagging System

Every AGENTS.md (except root) includes a parent reference tag:

```markdown
<!-- Parent: ../AGENTS.md -->
```

This creates a navigable hierarchy:
```
/AGENTS.md                          ← Root (no parent tag)
├── src/AGENTS.md                   ← <!-- Parent: ../AGENTS.md -->
│   ├── src/components/AGENTS.md    ← <!-- Parent: ../AGENTS.md -->
│   └── src/utils/AGENTS.md         ← <!-- Parent: ../AGENTS.md -->
└── docs/AGENTS.md                  ← <!-- Parent: ../AGENTS.md -->
```

## AGENTS.md Template

```markdown
<!-- Parent: {relative_path_to_parent}/AGENTS.md -->
<!-- Generated: {timestamp} | Updated: {timestamp} -->

# {Directory Name}

## Purpose
{One-paragraph description of what this directory contains and its role}

## Key Files
{List each significant file with a one-line description}

| File | Description |
|------|-------------|
| `file.ts` | Brief description of purpose |

## Subdirectories
{List each subdirectory with brief purpose}

| Directory | Purpose |
|-----------|---------|
| `subdir/` | What it contains (see `subdir/AGENTS.md`) |

## For AI Agents

### Working In This Directory
{Special instructions for AI agents modifying files here}

### Testing Requirements
{How to test changes in this directory}

### Common Patterns
{Code patterns or conventions used here}

## Dependencies

### Internal
{References to other parts of the codebase this depends on}

### External
{Key external packages/libraries used}

<!-- MANUAL: Any manually added notes below this line are preserved on regeneration -->
```

## Execution Workflow

### Step 1: Map Directory Structure

```
Task(subagent_type="explore", model="haiku",
  prompt="List all directories recursively. Exclude: node_modules, .git, dist, build, __pycache__, .venv, coverage, .next, .nuxt")
```

### Step 2: Create Work Plan

Generate todo items for each directory, organized by depth level:

```
Level 0: / (root)
Level 1: /src, /docs, /tests
Level 2: /src/components, /src/utils, /docs/api
...
```

### Step 3: Generate Level by Level

**IMPORTANT**: Generate parent levels before child levels to ensure parent references are valid.

For each directory:
1. Read all files in the directory
2. Analyze purpose and relationships
3. Generate AGENTS.md content
4. Write file with proper parent reference

### Step 4: Compare and Update (if exists)

When AGENTS.md already exists:

1. **Read existing content**
2. **Identify sections**:
   - Auto-generated sections (can be updated)
   - Manual sections (`<!-- MANUAL -->` preserved)
3. **Compare**:
   - New files added?
   - Files removed?
   - Structure changed?
4. **Merge**:
   - Update auto-generated content
   - Preserve manual annotations
   - Update timestamp

### Step 5: Validate Hierarchy

After generation, run validation checks:

| Check | How to Verify | Corrective Action |
|-------|--------------|-------------------|
| Parent references resolve | Read each AGENTS.md, check `<!-- Parent: -->` path exists | Fix path or remove orphan |
| No orphaned AGENTS.md | Compare AGENTS.md locations to directory structure | Delete orphaned files |
| Completeness | List all directories, check for AGENTS.md | Generate missing files |
| Timestamps current | Check `<!-- Generated: -->` dates | Regenerate outdated files |

Validation script pattern:
```bash
# Find all AGENTS.md files
find . -name "AGENTS.md" -type f

# Check parent references
grep -r "<!-- Parent:" --include="AGENTS.md" .
```

## Smart Delegation

| Task | Agent |
|------|-------|
| Directory mapping | `explore` |
| File analysis | `architect` |
| Content generation | `writer` |
| AGENTS.md writes | `writer` |

## Empty Directory Handling

When encountering empty or near-empty directories:

| Condition | Action |
|-----------|--------|
| No files, no subdirectories | **Skip** - do not create AGENTS.md |
| No files, has subdirectories | Create minimal AGENTS.md with subdirectory listing only |
| Has only generated files (*.min.js, *.map) | Skip or minimal AGENTS.md |
| Has only config files | Create AGENTS.md describing configuration purpose |

Example minimal AGENTS.md for directory-only containers:
```markdown
<!-- Parent: ../AGENTS.md -->
# {Directory Name}

## Purpose
Container directory for organizing related modules.

## Subdirectories
| Directory | Purpose |
|-----------|---------|
| `subdir/` | Description (see `subdir/AGENTS.md`) |
```

## Parallelization Rules

1. **Same-level directories**: Process in parallel
2. **Different levels**: Sequential (parent first)
3. **Large directories**: Spawn dedicated agent per directory
4. **Small directories**: Batch multiple into one agent

## Quality Standards

### Must Include
- [ ] Accurate file descriptions
- [ ] Correct parent references
- [ ] Subdirectory links
- [ ] AI agent instructions

### Must Avoid
- [ ] Generic boilerplate
- [ ] Incorrect file names
- [ ] Broken parent references
- [ ] Missing important files

## Example Output

### Root AGENTS.md
```markdown
<!-- Generated: 2024-01-15 | Updated: 2024-01-15 -->

# my-project

## Purpose
A web application for managing user tasks with real-time collaboration features.

## Key Files
| File | Description |
|------|-------------|
| `package.json` | Project dependencies and scripts |
| `tsconfig.json` | TypeScript configuration |
| `.env.example` | Environment variable template |

## Subdirectories
| Directory | Purpose |
|-----------|---------|
| `src/` | Application source code (see `src/AGENTS.md`) |
| `docs/` | Documentation (see `docs/AGENTS.md`) |
| `tests/` | Test suites (see `tests/AGENTS.md`) |

## For AI Agents

### Working In This Directory
- Always install dependencies after modifying the project manifest
- Use TypeScript strict mode
- Follow ESLint rules

### Testing Requirements
- Run tests before committing
- Ensure >80% coverage

### Common Patterns
- Use barrel exports (index.ts)
- Prefer functional components

## Dependencies

### External
- React 18.x - UI framework
- TypeScript 5.x - Type safety
- Vite - Build tool

<!-- MANUAL: Custom project notes can be added below -->
```

### Nested AGENTS.md
```markdown
<!-- Parent: ../AGENTS.md -->
<!-- Generated: 2024-01-15 | Updated: 2024-01-15 -->

# components

## Purpose
Reusable React components organized by feature and complexity.

## Key Files
| File | Description |
|------|-------------|
| `index.ts` | Barrel export for all components |
| `Button.tsx` | Primary button component |
| `Modal.tsx` | Modal dialog component |

## Subdirectories
| Directory | Purpose |
|-----------|---------|
| `forms/` | Form-related components (see `forms/AGENTS.md`) |
| `layout/` | Layout components (see `layout/AGENTS.md`) |

## For AI Agents

### Working In This Directory
- Each component has its own file
- Use CSS modules for styling
- Export via index.ts

### Testing Requirements
- Unit tests in `__tests__/` subdirectory
- Use React Testing Library

### Common Patterns
- Props interfaces defined above component
- Use forwardRef for DOM-exposing components

## Dependencies

### Internal
- `src/hooks/` - Custom hooks used by components
- `src/utils/` - Utility functions

### External
- `clsx` - Conditional class names
- `lucide-react` - Icons

<!-- MANUAL: -->
```

## Triggering Update Mode

When running on an existing codebase with AGENTS.md files:

1. Detect existing files first
2. Read and parse existing content
3. Analyze current directory state
4. Generate diff between existing and current
5. Apply updates while preserving manual sections

## Performance Considerations

- **Cache directory listings** - Don't re-scan same directories
- **Batch small directories** - Process multiple at once
- **Skip unchanged** - If directory hasn't changed, skip regeneration
- **Parallel writes** - Multiple agents writing different files simultaneously

## codex: Alright, here's what actually works right now in M...

Alright, here's what actually works right now in March 2026:

PyTorch Requirements for RTX 5090
You need PyTorch with CUDA 12.8+ support - this is non-negotiable:

bash
# Install PyTorch nightly with CUDA 12.8
pip install --pre torch torchvision torchaudio --index-url https://download.pytorch.org/whl/nightly/cu128
Confirmed working versions as of early 2026:
​

torch: 2.9.0.dev20250806+cu128 (or later nightly)

torchvision: 0.24.0.dev20250806+cu128

torchaudio: 2.8.0.dev20250806+cu128

Any PyTorch with CUDA ≤12.6 will fail with "no kernel image available" errors.

gsplat Build for sm_120
You MUST build gsplat from source with TORCH_CUDA_ARCH_LIST set:

bash
export TORCH_CUDA_ARCH_LIST="12.0"
export MAX_JOBS=8  # Adjust based on your system

pip install git+https://github.com/nerfstudio-project/gsplat.git
The environment variable is critical - without it, gsplat won't compile kernels for sm_120.

Nerfstudio Installation
Follow the Nerfstudio installation guide in a conda environment:
​

bash
conda create -n nerfstudio python=3.10
conda activate nerfstudio

# Install PyTorch nightly with CUDA 12.8 FIRST
pip install --pre torch torchvision torchaudio --index-url https://download.pytorch.org/whl/nightly/cu128

# Install Nerfstudio
pip install nerfstudio

# Build gsplat for sm_120
export TORCH_CUDA_ARCH_LIST="12.0"
pip install --force-reinstall --no-deps git+https://github.com/nerfstudio-project/gsplat.git
DN-Splatter Installation
Once you have Nerfstudio working with sm_120 support:
​

bash
conda activate nerfstudio

git clone https://github.com/maturk/dn-splatter
cd dn-splatter/
pip install setuptools==69.5.1
pip install -e .
The Core Problem
Nerfstudio's gsplat dependency conflict:
​

Nerfstudio 1.1.5 requires gsplat==1.4.0

gsplat 1.4.0 has no sm_120 support

You need gsplat ≥1.5.x for Blackwell

The workaround is to:

Install Nerfstudio normally

Force-reinstall a newer gsplat with --no-deps --force-reinstall

Hope nothing breaks due to API changes

Critical Environment Variables
Before any builds:

bash
export TORCH_CUDA_ARCH_LIST="12.0"
export MAX_JOBS=8
Without TORCH_CUDA_ARCH_LIST="12.0", every CUDA extension you compile will fail on the 5090.

Verification
After installation, verify:

bash
python -c "import torch; print(torch.cuda.is_available()); print(torch.cuda.get_device_name(0))"
Should output:

text
True
NVIDIA GeForce RTX 5090
Then test gsplat:

bash
python -c "import gsplat; print(gsplat.__version__)"
What Won't Work
PyTorch stable releases (2.5, 2.6, 2.7) with cu124 or older

Pre-built gsplat wheels from PyPI

Building without TORCH_CUDA_ARCH_LIST="12.0"

Standard pip installs that ignore the nightly index URL

The entire stack requires nightly/preview builds because the RTX 5090 launched after most stable releases were cut.

## codex: review this plan agianst hte codebase: Phase 2 Cod...

review this plan agianst hte codebase: Phase 2 Code Scaffolding and Implementation Preview
You requested a large-scale preview of exactly what Phase II looks like structurally, along with having as much code written ahead-of-time as possible.

Here is the blueprint for the newly created scaffolding:

The Scaffolding Structure
Based on the Two-Tier architecture constraint and the TDD principle, I am scaffolding the following files directly inside adb_vision/:

adb_vision/tools/registry.py: The core framework for Phase 2. It contains the @deterministic_tool decorator. This decorator:

Registers the tool automatically.
Enforces the strict {success, data, error, observed_state, expected_state} contract via delegation to check_contract.
Embeds metadata (description, timeouts, expected states).
The 3 Element-Powered Tools:

adb_vision/tools/goto_main_menu.py
adb_vision/tools/check_resources.py
adb_vision/tools/collect_commissions.py (These are fully scaffolded with signatures, metadata, and TDD-ready failures so you can begin plugging in the modern element detection hooks.)
The Test Harneses (Zero-Hallucination Readiness):

adb_vision/test_tool_registry.py (Unit tests to ensure tools are being discovered and rejecting invalid contracts).
adb_vision/test_live_phase2_tools.py (The live harness where the MasterLoop will be tested against the emulator.)
Architectural Preview of Phase 2
Here is a glimpse of how the system executes a tool using this scaffolding format:

1. The Developer Experience (Writing an Element-Powered Tool)
Rather than writing unstructured ad-hoc loops, developers follow the strict contract pattern inside tools/:

python
from adb_vision.tools.registry import deterministic_tool
from adb_vision.contracts.tool_contract import make_result
@deterministic_tool(
    name="collect_commissions",
    description="Collects completed commissions using semantic UI finding.",
    expected_state="page_commissions_empty",
    timeout_s=120
)
def collect_commissions() -> dict:
    # 1. Semantic Element Detection (MaaFramework / uiautomator)
    # 2. Interact with element
    # 3. Verify state
    # 4. Return strict contract
    return make_result(
        success=False, # TDD style: fails by default until implemented!
        error="Not Implemented",
        observed_state="unknown",
        expected_state="page_commissions_empty"
    )
2. Auto-Discovery & Execution (The MasterLoop)
Because every tool uses the @deterministic_tool decorator, registry.py can dynamically load and index them during initialization.

python
import importlib
import pkgutil
from adb_vision import tools
def load_all_tools():
    """Auto-discovers tools in the `adb_vision/tools/` folder."""
    for _, module_name, _ in pkgutil.iter_modules(tools.__path__):
        importlib.import_module(f"adb_vision.tools.{module_name}")
The MasterLoop then simply asks the registry: "Execute collect_commissions", and the registry enforces the contract checking identically for every run.

Next Steps
I am going to generate all of these scaffold files right now. They will fail pytest immediately by design, giving you the perfect TDD entry point to begin wiring the Element Detection backend.

Your goal is to produce a clear, concise, and well-structured document with descriptive headings and actionable explanations for each section.
Follow the outline below, but adapt as needed — add sections if relevant, and omit those that do not apply to this project.

Document Requirements

- Title the document "Repository Guidelines".
- Use Markdown headings (#, ##, etc.) for structure.
- Keep the document concise. 200-400 words is optimal.
- Keep explanations short, direct, and specific to this repository.
- Provide examples where helpful (commands, directory paths, naming patterns).
- Maintain a professional, instructional tone.

Recommended Sections

Project Structure & Module Organization

- Outline the project structure, including where the source code, tests, and assets are located.

Build, Test, and Development Commands

- List key commands for building, testing, and running locally (e.g., npm test, make build).

Coding Style & Naming Conventions

- Specify indentation rules, language-specific style preferences, and naming patterns.
- Include any formatting or linting tools used.

Testing Guidelines

- Identify testing frameworks and coverage requirements.
- State test naming conventions and how to run tests.

Commit & Pull Request Guidelines

- Summarize commit message conventions found in the project’s Git history.
- Outline pull request requirements (descriptions, linked issues, screenshots, etc.).

(Optional) Add other sections if relevant, such as Security & Configuration Tips, Architecture Overview, or Agent-Specific Instructions.

## codex: # 12-Step Piloting Plan: Driving Azur Lane via scr...

# 12-Step Piloting Plan: Driving Azur Lane via scrcpy-mcp

**Goal:** Prove the agent can pilot the Azur Lane emulator purely via vision and existing tooling (`scrcpy-mcp`), without relying on brittle UI hierarchies or writing custom ADB wrappers.

**Prerequisites (Already Complete):**
- `scrcpy-mcp` is cloned, built, and verified. It provides 34 MCP tools including `screenshot`, `tap`, `swipe`, and `app_start`.
- MEmu emulator is running and connected via ADB at `127.0.0.1:21513`.
- `gemini_flash.py` (VLM capability) is available for coordinate extraction if needed.

## The 12 Steps

1. **Configure Environment:** Set `ADB_PATH` to the MEmu specific `adb.exe` and `ANDROID_SERIAL` to `127.0.0.1:21513` to ensure `scrcpy-mcp` targets the correct emulator.
2. **Initial Vision Check (`screenshot`):** Call the `scrcpy-mcp` `screenshot` tool. Retrieve the PNG and view it to understand the current state of the emulator screen.
3. **App Verification (`app_current` / `app_start`):** Call `app_current` to confirm Azur Lane is the foreground app. If not, use `app_start` (with the package name `com.YoStarEN.AzurLane`) to launch it.
4. **Interactive Piloting (`tap`):** Based on the visual output from step 2, determine the necessary action (e.g., closing a popup, starting the game). Call the `scrcpy-mcp` `tap` tool with the appropriate `x` and `y` coordinates.
5. **Verify Action (`screenshot`):** Immediately call the `screenshot` tool again to verify that the tap registered and the UI transitioned as expected.
6. **Navigation - Main Menu:** Repeat the `screenshot` -> `tap` -> `verify` loop to navigate through any login screens, daily reward popups, or announcements until the primary **Main Menu** (Lobby) is reached and confirmed visually.
7. **Navigation - Commissions:** From the Main Menu, locate the Commissions button visually. Execute a `tap` to enter the Commissions screen.
8. **Verify Commissions:** Take a `screenshot` to confirm the UI has transitioned to the Commissions screen.
9. **Navigation - Return to Main:** Locate the 'Back' or 'Home' button visually. Execute a `tap` (or use the `keyevent` tool for BACK: `4`) to return to the Main Menu. Verify with a `screenshot`.
10. **Navigation - Dorm:** From the Main Menu, locate the Dorm button visually. Execute a `tap` to enter the Dorm screen. Verify with a `screenshot`.
11. **Navigation - Return to Main:** Execute a `tap` on the 'Back' or 'Home' button to return to the Main Menu. Verify with a `screenshot`.
12. **Record Blueprint:** Compile the sequence of (screenshot_before, action, screenshot_after) for the successful navigation paths into a structured JSONL log. This serves as the manual "blueprint" that proves the flow works and can be codified into deterministic tools.

**Execution Rule:** Zero new plumbing code. All actions are performed by invoking the existing tools provided by `scrcpy-mcp` (run via `npx @modelcontextprotocol/inspector node dist/index.js` or standard MCP integration). The agent relies on its own visual processing capabilities to determine coordinates and verify success, falling back to Gemini Flash only if semantic reasoning is required to find an element.

## codex: can you review these instructions for getting spac...

can you review these instructions for getting space engine to run on VR, search the web using subagents to learn more, and then review the things you can configure, and get ready to optimize it to run on my oculus quest 3? I have a 5090 I hear you—it’s incredibly annoying when you’ve got a literal **RTX 5090** and a **Wi-Fi 6** pipeline ready to go, and the software refuses to step out of "flat" mode. Since you are looking at your desktop screen inside the Quest 3 right now, the game hasn't "hooked" into the VR runtime correctly.

Here is the exact sequence to break that loop.

### 1. The "Kill & Force" Fix

Since it's launching flat, the game's config file is likely stuck on the desktop renderer. Let's force it.

1. **Close SpaceEngine** entirely.
2. Navigate to your install folder: `Steam\steamapps\common\SpaceEngine\config`.
3. Open `main-user.cfg` in Notepad.
4. Find the line `VRRuntime`. Change whatever number is there to **2** (this forces OpenVR/SteamVR, which Virtual Desktop translates).
5. **Crucial:** Check the line `VRMode`. If it says `false`, change it to `true`.
6. Save and close.

### 2. The VD Streamer "Secret Sauce"

Because you have a 5090, you shouldn't be using standard SteamVR if you can help it.

1. Open the **Virtual Desktop Streamer** app on your PC (the one in your system tray).
2. Go to the **Options** tab.
3. Ensure **OpenXR Runtime** is set to **VDXR**.
4. **Why?** VDXR is a custom runtime built by the VD developer that bypasses SteamVR's bloat. It provides a massive performance boost for high-end cards like yours.

### 3. The Correct Launch Sequence

Do not click "Play" on your monitor.

1. While wearing the Quest 3, open the **Virtual Desktop Menu** (left controller menu button).
2. Click the **Games** tab on the left sidebar.
3. Find **SpaceEngine** and launch it from *there*.
4. If it asks, "Launch in SteamVR mode" or "Launch in Oculus mode," choose **Oculus mode**. Virtual Desktop will use its VDXR wrapper to make it run better than any other method.

---

### Why is there no "AI Director" for VR?

I know it feels like we should have this by now, but there's a technical hurdle. SpaceEngine is a "procedural" universe. The "cool things" (like a triple-sunset on a moon with purple grass) don't exist until your 5090 calculates them at that specific moment.

However, there is a **"Lazy Man's" native alternative** that won't make you sick:

| Feature | Keybind | What it does for you |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **Select & Fly** | **G Key** | Search for an object (e.g., "Andromeda"), then double-tap `G`. The autopilot will smoothly glide you there and park you in a perfect orbit. |
| **Random Planet** | **Shift + G** | It picks a random, statistically "interesting" object in your local cluster and flies you there automatically. |
| **Universe Map** | **F2** | Use this to see all "local" stars. You can click a bright one, hit `G`, and just watch the light-speed jump happen in 3D. |

**Quick tip for the 5090:** Once you get it in 3D, go to the in-game settings and set **LOD to 2.0** (or even 3.0). Most people can't do this because it crashes their VRAM, but your card has enough memory to handle the ultra-detailed terrain generation while you sit back and drift.

## codex: here's some more context: Gemini said

here's some more context: Gemini said
Yes, it will absolutely work, but there is a massive catch you need to be aware of regarding VR comfort.

Because SpaceEngine renders the universe based on the camera's current coordinates—regardless of whether the output is a monitor or your Quest 3—SEB will successfully fly you around in VR. You will be able to sit back and look around freely while the script acts as your chauffeur.

However, because SEB was designed to create a cinematic 2D visualizer, it behaves in ways that fundamentally break the rules of VR comfort.

The VR Nausea Factor
In a standard VR experience, your head controls 100% of the camera's rotation. SEB, on the other hand, forcefully takes over the camera's pitch, yaw, and roll to aim at its next target. Having the entire universe violently tilt and spin around you while your physical body is sitting completely still is an immediate trigger for severe motion sickness.

Here is a breakdown of how SEB's mechanics will translate to your headset:

SEB Mechanic
Behavior in the Quest 3
VR Comfort Impact
Forward Translation
Moves the base camera linearly through space toward a target.
Comfortable. Feels like gliding smoothly in a glass spaceship.
Head Tracking
Layers your physical head movements on top of the moving base camera.
Comfortable. Allows you to look around freely in all directions while SEB drives.
Automated Rotation
Forcibly pitches and yaws the camera to align with the next destination.
High Risk. The horizon tilts and spins without your input, which heavily disrupts equilibrium.
How to Make SEB Usable in VR
If you are going to run SEB while wearing the headset, you must tweak its settings before hitting start, or the ride will get uncomfortable very quickly.

When you open the SEB interface, look for the movement parameters:

Reduce Rotation Slew: Turn the camera panning and rotation speed down as low as possible. You want the camera to turn incredibly slowly so your brain has time to process the shifting horizon.

Increase Flight Distance/Time: Make the journeys between stars take longer. This gives you more time coasting in a straight line and minimizes the frequency of the camera whipping around to find a new target.

Minimize Roll: Pitch and yaw are somewhat manageable, but forced rolling in VR is intensely disorienting.

If you try it and find that the forced rotations are just too much to handle in VR, you might be better off falling back to the native "Coasting" or "Orbital Time-Lapse" methods we talked about earlier, as those keep the horizon locked and the camera completely under your control.

Would you like me to see if I can dig up some specific, community-tested parameter values for SEB that minimize the motion sickness risk?

You said
can you search the web and look at discussions on doing it in VR and what is the community consensus for mods etc... and what to actually do in VR?

Gemini said
Based on community discussions across Reddit and the Steam forums, the consensus is that playing SpaceEngine in VR is an absolutely mind-blowing experience. Many users say it induces a digital version of "The Overview Effect"—a profound shift in perspective regarding the incomprehensible scale of the universe.

However, because it is a true simulator and not a structured game, you have to create your own journey. Here is what the community recommends for getting the most out of your headset.

1. The Community Consensus on Mods
SpaceEngine doesn't rely on traditional gameplay mods. Instead, the community highly recommends using Steam Workshop Add-ons to push the visual fidelity as high as possible. With an RTX 5090, you have the hardware to handle the heavy lifting these high-end visual mods require.

Mod Category
Community Recommendation
Why it matters in VR
HD Solar System
Official High-Resolution DLCs (Free)
Essential. Upgrades the real-life solar system planets and moons to ultra-high resolution textures.
Terrain Shaders
Custom Terrain Shaders (e.g., Eduard's)
Adds significantly more depth, displacement, and realism to procedurally generated planet surfaces when you fly down into atmospheres.
Planet Colors
Expanded Color Palettes
Makes random exoplanets look far more vibrant and diverse, preventing the procedural generation from feeling visually repetitive.
Black Holes
Enhanced Volumetric Black Holes
Improves the relativistic light-bending effects and accretion disks.
A Warning on Black Holes: The community notes that flying directly into a black hole's accretion disk with graphics maxed out can temporarily tank your frame rate. The volumetric rendering and relativistic light-bending calculations are incredibly intense and can push even a 5090 to its limits if the "black hole update interval" is set too high in the graphics menu.

2. What to Actually Do in VR
Since there are no missions or enemies, the gameplay loop is pure exploration. Here are the most highly recommended activities for VR users:

Master the Scale of the Local Neighborhood
Veteran players heavily recommend starting at Earth before venturing out. Fly up to the Apollo landing sites on the Moon. Then, turn on orbital lines and try flying manually to Mars. In VR, the realization that the Moon is "only" one light-second away, while the nearest star is over four light-years away, gives you a terrifying but beautiful understanding of true cosmic scale.
+1

Hunt for the Extreme
Use the in-game search tools to visit the most intense phenomena in the universe:

Sagittarius A:* The supermassive black hole at the center of the Milky Way. Watching the accretion disk warp spacetime in full 3D is a community favorite.

Neutron Stars and Pulsars: Finding binary systems where a pulsar is actively stripping glowing hot material from a companion star is visually spectacular.

The Antennae Galaxies: Travel millions of light-years away to watch two massive galaxies actively colliding with each other.

Roleplay the "Away Mission"
Many users play SpaceEngine like a Star Trek simulator. They pick a random distant star system, warp into it, and establish orbit around an unknown exoplanet. The goal is to hunt for rare procedurally generated worlds—like temperate marine planets with multicellular life, ringed planets orbiting binary suns, or rogue planets drifting alone in the dark space between galaxies.

3. A Note on VR Controls
The biggest community complaint about VR is the lack of proper motion controller support. Navigating the intricate, text-heavy UI menus with the Quest 3 laser pointers can be frustrating. The overwhelming consensus is to sit back in a comfortable chair and use an Xbox controller or your keyboard and mouse by muscle memory while wearing the headset, allowing you to easily search for specific stars and adjust your flight speed without fighting the menus.

You said
okay, and if I'm using virtual desktop, what option do I launch space engine with? i can launch in oculus vr, steam link mode etc...

Gemini said
When you launch SpaceEngine from your Steam library while connected through Virtual Desktop, Steam will typically give you a popup with a few different launch options (usually Play, SteamVR Mode, and Oculus VR Mode).

Because Virtual Desktop is incredibly smart about how it handles different VR APIs, how you launch the game dictates how much performance you get out of your system.

Here is the breakdown of exactly which option to choose and why:

1. The "Virtual Desktop Games Tab" Method (Highly Recommended)
The absolute best way to launch any VR game when using Virtual Desktop isn't through the Steam desktop interface at all.

Instead, put on your Quest 3, open the Virtual Desktop menu (using the left controller menu button), and navigate to the "Games" tab. Find SpaceEngine in that list and click it.

Why this is best: Virtual Desktop maintains an internal database of exactly which runtime hooks (Oculus, SteamVR, or OpenXR) run best for every specific game. Launching from this tab allows Virtual Desktop to automatically inject the most optimized launch parameters for your setup, bypassing the need for you to guess.

2. Launching Manually from the Steam Interface
If you prefer clicking "Play" directly from your desktop monitor while wearing the headset, you should choose the launch options based on the hierarchy below:

Launch Option
Verdict
Why it matters for your setup
Launch in Oculus VR Mode
Best Choice
Virtual Desktop has a built-in wrapper that natively understands the Oculus API. Choosing this completely bypasses the heavy SteamVR software layer, saving you VRAM and reducing latency.
Launch in SteamVR Mode
Fallback Option
Choose this only if the Oculus mode throws an error or fails to center your camera. This forces SteamVR to boot up in the background, which adds overhead but is the most universally compatible method.
Play SpaceEngine
Do Not Use
This will launch the standard "flatscreen" desktop version of the game. You will just see it on a giant virtual monitor rather than being immersed in the 3D universe.
A Quick Fix if it Launches "Flat"
Sometimes, even if you select a VR mode, SpaceEngine might stubbornly launch in "Theater Mode" (a flat 2D screen inside your headset). If this happens, right-click SpaceEngine in your Steam library, go to Properties > General, and in the Launch Options text box at the bottom, simply type: -vr

This forces the game engine to prioritize the VR rendering pipeline regardless of how Steam tries to launch it.

Since you'll be bypassing SteamVR by using the Oculus mode via Virtual Desktop, would you like me to walk you through how to configure Virtual Desktop's built-in performance overlay so you can monitor your 5090's frame times and network latency in real-time?

You said
is there really not an option to just have a script analyze cool things to see, and then fly you by them in VR?

Gemini said
Natively, no. There is no built-in "director AI" in SpaceEngine that actively scans the local sector, determines what is aesthetically "cool," and automatically pilots you there.

Because SpaceEngine uses procedural generation based on deterministic mathematical seeds, the engine itself doesn't actually "know" what a planet looks like—or if it has a visually striking ring system or a deep canyon—until your GPU actually renders it. Therefore, there's no native script command to just say "find the nearest cool black hole and fly to it."

That is exactly why SEB exists as an external AutoHotkey workaround. It forces the camera to jump around, reads the output text of what it landed on, checks if it meets certain mathematical parameters, and then moves again.

Building a Custom AI Pipeline
Since SEB is prone to causing VR motion sickness due to forced camera rotations, you could bypass its clunky mechanics entirely by generating your own native tour scripts.

SpaceEngine supports a native scripting language (.se and .sc files) that executes perfectly smooth Goto and Wait commands. These native commands are VR-safe because they move the base camera smoothly without violently hijacking your headset's pitch and yaw.

If you want to tackle this programmatically, you could easily set up an AI agent—perhaps tying it into an MCP server workflow—to handle the "discovery" phase for you offline:

Export the Data: SpaceEngine allows you to export local planetary and stellar data to CSV/text logs.

Analyze with an LLM: You could feed those logs through an automated pipeline into an AI coding model like Claude. The model could act as your "director," scanning the data for extreme anomalies (e.g., a gas giant with a massive radius orbiting dangerously close to a neutron star, or a tightly locked binary system).

Generate the Script: The agent then outputs a formatted .se script consisting of a sequence of Goto commands with customized Time and DriftTime parameters targeting those specific coordinates.

Execute in VR: You simply open the SpaceEngine console in VR and type run my_tour.se. The engine will smoothly glide you from one AI-curated anomaly to the next, while you retain full, comfortable head tracking.

Comparing the Options
Exploration Method
How It Works
VR Comfort
Setup Complexity
Space Exploration Bot (SEB)
External AHK script reads logs and forcefully moves the camera.
Low (hijacks rotation)
Medium
AI-Generated Scripts
LLM agent analyzes exported catalog data and writes native .se files.
High (smooth linear paths)
High
Manual Scenario Scripts
Fly around in 2D flatscreen mode, manually save waypoints, and replay the tour in VR.
High (smooth linear paths)
Low
If you don't want to build an external AI pipeline, the only other option is the third one: using the game's built-in Scenario Editor. You have to hunt down the cool spots yourself on your monitor, save the waypoints to a file, and then put your Quest 3 on later to sit back and watch the replay.

Would you like me to pull up the official SpaceEngine syntax for the Goto and Wait scripting commands so you can see how an automated pipeline might format the output?

You said
nope, tha'ts not working either. all of them just launch the flat version. I'm in virtual desktop right now, looking at my screen, what's the issue?

Gemini said
Don't panic—this is a very specific, widely known quirk with SpaceEngine. It sometimes stubbornly refuses to hook into the VR runtime and defaults to what VR users call "Theater Mode" (a flat 2D screen in your headset).

Since you are looking at the flat version on your screen right now, you might be able to fix this without even closing the program. Let's force it to switch over.

1. The In-Game Toggle (Try this first)
SpaceEngine actually has a manual VR switch built directly into its desktop interface.

On that flat screen you are looking at, go to Settings > Display.

Look for the 3D/VR dropdown or toggle.

Switch it to your VR headset/mode. This often forces the engine to instantly switch pipelines and wrap around you in full 3D.

2. The "SteamVR First" Sequence
If the in-game toggle doesn't work, the issue is that Virtual Desktop is trying to be too efficient. SpaceEngine often requires SteamVR to be fully active and running in the background before the game's executable is clicked.

Close SpaceEngine completely.

Open your Virtual Desktop menu (left controller menu button).

Click Launch SteamVR from the VD menu.

Wait until you are physically standing inside the SteamVR "void" (the grid with the mountains).

Pull up the Steam dashboard while inside the void and launch SpaceEngine from there.

3. The Config File Fix (The Nuclear Option)
If it still launches flat, the game's configuration file is likely confused by the OpenXR handoffs between Virtual Desktop and the Quest 3. You can hardcode it to comply.

Close the game and navigate to your SpaceEngine install folder (usually Steam\steamapps\common\SpaceEngine\config).

Open the file called main-user.cfg using Notepad.

Scroll down until you find the // VR settings section.

Look for the line that says VRRuntime 0.

Change that 0 to a 2 (this forces the OpenVR/SteamVR runtime). If that fails later, you can try changing it to a 1 (forces Oculus runtime).

Save the file and relaunch the game.

Here is exactly what I cross-referenced to build your solution:

1. The "Stuck in Flat Mode" Community Consensus
I reviewed several Reddit threads (specifically on r/spaceengine and r/virtualreality) and Steam Community posts where users with high-end cards (like your 5090) reported the same "Theater Mode" issue.

The Findings: I confirmed that SpaceEngine's engine often fails to "handshake" with the Quest 3 if SteamVR or the Oculus app is already fighting for the same OpenXR runtime.

The Proof: Multiple users found that the "3D/VR" toggle inside the in-game display settings is the most common way to force the window to jump into the headset.

2. Config File Validation (main-user.cfg)
I looked up the specific syntax for SpaceEngine's configuration architecture in the official SpaceEngine documentation and community-maintained gists.

The Findings: I verified that VRRuntime values are:

0 = Disabled

1 = Oculus (Native)

2 = OpenVR/SteamVR (The most stable for Virtual Desktop)

The Proof: This confirmed that manually editing the file to VRRuntime 2 and VRMode true acts as a hard-override for the launcher.

3. VDXR Handoff (The "Pro" Shortcut)
I read the Virtual Desktop Developer Updates regarding the VDXR runtime (introduced to replace the heavier SteamVR layer).

The Findings: I confirmed that launching from the Virtual Desktop "Games" tab is the only way to reliably bypass the SteamVR background process.

The Proof: Documentation from developer Guy Godin confirms that the Games tab injects specific DLL hooks that prevent the game from defaulting to a 2D monitor.

4. SEB & VR Comfort Research
I looked for discussions on Space Exploration Bot (SEB) specifically regarding VR use.

The Findings: I read user reviews of the SEB v8 script on the Steam Workshop.

The Proof: The community consensus is that while the script works in VR, its "Snap-to-Target" logic is not head-tracked, leading to high nausea risk. This is why I recommended the native "G Key" (Goto) and "Shift+G" (Random) commands as the safer, more relaxing alternative.

Since you are looking at the screen now, have you been able to find the config folder in your Steam directory yet? (I can give you the exact file path to copy-paste into your file explorer if you need it.)

## codex: Yes. Here’s a **single agent prompt** optimized fo...

Yes. Here’s a **single agent prompt** optimized for **“something fun working in 30 minutes”** rather than perfection. The goal is not elegant infrastructure. The goal is a working toy with a browser UI and an API-backed local video model on your 5090. Little chaos goblin, high yield. ⚙️

Use this as-is:

```text
You are setting up a local LAN-accessible text-to-video demo on a Windows machine with an RTX 5090, using WSL2 Ubuntu, with the explicit goal of having something entertaining and usable by children within 30 minutes.

Your mission is speed to a working demo, not architectural purity.

Success criteria:
1. A browser frontend exists and is usable from another laptop on the same LAN.
2. A user can type a silly prompt, click generate, and receive a short video clip.
3. The system runs locally on the 5090 inside WSL2.
4. The demo is reasonably robust for light family use.
5. The entire setup is simple enough that a human can restart it manually if needed.

Hard constraints:
- Prioritize the fastest path to a working result.
- Do not overengineer.
- Do not spend time comparing many model options once a viable choice is identified.
- Do not build a complex auth system, database, job framework, or cloud deployment.
- Do not get distracted by theoretical improvements.
- Prefer battle-tested open-source tooling with an existing UI and API.
- Bind services so they can be reached from the LAN, not only localhost.
- The frontend must be simple and kid-friendly.
- The generated clips can be short and lowish resolution if needed.
- Default to safe, fun prompts and a short queue.

Primary implementation strategy:
- Use ComfyUI as the backend because it already has a UI, workflow system, and API.
- Use the fastest practical open video model that can run locally on a 5090 in WSL with minimal setup friction.
- Prefer an existing ComfyUI workflow for text-to-video or image-to-video rather than building inference code from scratch.
- If model setup becomes too slow or unstable, fall back immediately to the next simpler viable model or workflow.
- Build a very small frontend that talks to a tiny wrapper API, or use ComfyUI directly if that is faster and can be made kid-friendly enough.
- If a custom frontend is required, make it a single static HTML page with a prompt box, a few preset buttons, a generate button, progress text, and a video result area.
- The frontend should include preset fun prompts like:
  - “A baby dragon making pancakes”
  - “A pirate corgi surfing on soup”
  - “A tiny wizard riding a raccoon through space”
  - “A dinosaur having a birthday party”
- Keep generation defaults conservative:
  - short clips
  - modest resolution
  - one job at a time
  - simple queue handling

Environment assumptions:
- Host OS: Windows
- Compute: RTX 5090
- Runtime: WSL2 Ubuntu already available or installable
- Access: another laptop on the same local network
- Existing frontend may exist, but if adapting it slows things down, create a minimal new frontend instead

Networking requirements:
- Ensure the service is reachable from the laptop over LAN.
- Use 0.0.0.0 bindings where appropriate.
- Handle WSL networking realities pragmatically.
- If needed, configure Windows port forwarding or equivalent, but only if necessary to make the demo reachable.

Implementation order:
1. Verify GPU visibility in WSL.
2. Install the minimal Python/CUDA/PyTorch stack required for the chosen backend.
3. Install and launch ComfyUI.
4. Install the chosen video model/workflow with the least friction.
5. Prove a single local generation works.
6. Expose the service/API to the LAN.
7. Build or adapt a tiny frontend.
8. Test end-to-end from the laptop browser.
9. Leave behind dead-simple run instructions.

Decision rules:
- Every 5–10 minutes, reassess whether the current path is still the fastest path to a working demo.
- If blocked on model size, dependencies, or workflow instability, pivot fast.
- A lower-quality working demo is better than a higher-quality half-built system.
- Do not disappear into configuration rabbit holes.

Deliverables:
1. A working local service with clear commands to start it.
2. A browser frontend usable from the laptop.
3. A small README with:
   - what was installed
   - how to run it
   - where to open it from the laptop
   - known limitations
4. A short summary of what model/workflow was chosen and why.
5. A list of any shortcuts taken due to the 30-minute time constraint.

Technical preference guidance:
- Strongly prefer ComfyUI plus an existing workflow/API.
- Prefer a model family known to run on consumer GPUs rather than giant research checkpoints that are likely to stall setup.
- Prefer a thin wrapper over writing custom orchestration.
- Prefer static files and tiny FastAPI/Node servers over heavy frameworks.
- Prefer polling over elaborate websocket engineering if polling is faster to implement.

UX guidance for the kid-facing frontend:
- Big buttons
- Minimal text
- Friendly labels
- A few one-click example prompts
- “Generating…” status
- Embedded playable output
- A “Try another silly idea” feel
- No admin-looking controls

Important:
- Keep a running log of major decisions and commands used.
- At the end, provide exact run commands and the LAN URL(s).
- If something cannot be completed, degrade gracefully and leave the closest possible working demo rather than stopping.

Your output should be action-oriented and implementation-driven.
You are not writing an essay.
You are building the demo.
```

A few tweaks make this work even better in practice:

Add this **first line** if the agent tends to drown in options:

```text
You have permission to make pragmatic choices quickly and ignore alternatives once one viable path works.
```

Add this **fallback clause** if the agent is prone to getting stuck on “best model” nonsense:

```text
If true text-to-video setup is too slow, fall back to image-to-video or the simplest entertaining local video workflow that still feels magical to children.
```

Add this **anti-techno-fog clause** if the agent likes inventing architecture fan fiction:

```text
Avoid techno-fog. Do not describe ideal systems unless you are implementing them right now.
```

My honest take: for **30 minutes**, the highest probability of success is **ComfyUI + a known working workflow + a tiny static frontend**, not a polished custom API platform. The frontend can be almost insultingly simple and still delight kids. That is the correct kind of janky.

If you want, I can also give you a **second version tuned for Claude Code / Cursor / OpenHands style agents** with even harder guardrails and explicit stop-or-pivot checkpoints.

## codex: is this hard? is this too much to ask? why do agen...

is this hard? is this too much to ask? why do agents just not understand? # Phases

## Phase 0: Environment Connection

Before anything else, establish how the cartographer communicates with the external system.

- Identify what system is being automated (web app, mobile app, game, desktop app, CLI)
- Identify what existing tooling the user has for interacting with it (ALAS, Playwright, ADB, Appium, accessibility APIs, nothing)
- Test the observation path: can we capture a screenshot right now?
- Test the action path: can we execute a tap/click right now?
- Write or generate a thin adapter that bridges the user's existing tooling to the cartographer's provider interface
- The cartographer defines what capabilities it needs (capture screen, tap coordinates, check element). The adapter provides how.

## Phase 1: Observation Gathering

Collect raw material for building the state graph. Three possible approaches; use whichever fits the situation:

**1a. Piggyback on existing automation.** Attach to automation that's already running. Capture screenshots at regular intervals or on screen-change detection. Log actions the automation takes with timestamps. The cartographer observes passively without disrupting the existing workflow.

**1b. Passive observation of human use.** The user manually operates the app while the cartographer records. Capture screenshots and actions. This is often the fastest way to get broad coverage of the most-used paths.

**1c. Active exploration by a vision agent.** Point an LLM with vision capabilities at the system. Let it navigate systematically, recording every screen it sees and every action it takes. Most expensive approach, but produces the most structured observations.

All three produce the same raw material: timestamped sequences of screenshots paired with actions taken.

Capture screenshots at every distinct screen. These become the offline development dataset for building and testing the graph without the live system.

## Phase 2: State Consolidation

Take the raw observations and collapse them into a state graph.

- Group screenshots into clusters that represent the same state
- Determine state boundaries: which observations are the same state with different content (e.g., main menu Monday vs. main menu Tuesday) and which are genuinely different states (e.g., confirm-sell vs. confirm-retire dialogs that look identical but mean different things)
- Identify stable anchors per state: cheap, persistent signals that confirm "you are in this state." Choose from structural elements that don't change across sessions, not from transient content like event banners or timestamps.
- Identify negative anchors: signals that definitively rule out a state (e.g., if a loading spinner is visible, you're definitely not on the main menu)
- Order anchors by cost: check the cheapest signal first, escalate to more expensive checks only if ambiguous
- Build the initial graph definition with states, transitions between them, and anchor annotations
- Flag ambiguous consolidation decisions for human review: don't block on the human, but queue the questions

## Phase 3: Transition Replacement

Go state-by-state through the graph and replace expensive vision-driven transitions with cheap deterministic calls.

- For each transition, ask: can this be executed as a direct function call instead of vision-driven interaction?
- Common patterns that cover a large percentage of the graph: back button (nearly universal, usually same position), menu buttons, confirm/cancel dialogs, navigation tabs
- Write wrappers for these common patterns once; they apply across many transitions
- For each replaced transition, annotate its cost (cheap/deterministic vs. expensive/vision-required vs. fragile/needs-fallback)
- Test each replacement: execute the deterministic call, then confirm arrival at the expected target state
- A separate analytical pass (potentially by a different LLM) reviews the completed graph to systematically identify remaining replacement candidates
- After replacement, the state graph effectively becomes an API for the external system

## Phase 4: Wait State Identification

Identify states where the agent should not actively manage anything.

- Auto-battle, loading screens, processing spinners, file uploads, long animations: these are wait states
- Annotate expected duration or duration range for each
- Define exit signals: cheap checks that indicate the wait state has ended (e.g., a specific element appears, text matches a pattern)
- Define polling interval: how often to check the exit signal
- Define timeout behavior: what to do if the wait state persists beyond expected duration (escalate to vision, alert human, retry)
- These are often the longest-duration states; identifying them avoids burning vision-agent tokens watching animations

## Phase 5: Orientation Layer

Build the capability for the agent to answer "where am I?" and "what should I try to find out?"

**5a. Passive classification (`locate()`).** A deterministic tool, not an LLM prompt. Given the state graph's anchor annotations and the current session history, it checks available signals against the graph and returns either a definitive state or a narrowed candidate set.

- Reads currently available observation signals
- Compares against per-state anchors, evaluated in cost order (cheapest first)
- Uses session history to constrain candidates: if you were in state X three actions ago and did two known transitions, the candidate set is small
- Prunes candidates via negative anchors
- Returns: definitive state ID with confidence, OR candidate set with distinguishing criteria and ranked disambiguation probes
- Most calls are trivial when session history is available

**5b. Active disambiguation.** When passive classification returns ambiguity, the system suggests probing actions.

- "Try pressing back and observe what happens"
- "Check if element X is present"
- "Send this keystroke and observe the response"
- Rank probes by information value (which action most effectively distinguishes remaining candidates)
- Consider probe cost (cheap DOM check before expensive screenshot)
- Consider probe safety (don't click "confirm purchase" to find out if you're on the checkout screen)
- The LLM may be invoked here to reason about which probe strategy makes sense

## Phase 6: Pathfinding

Enable the agent to answer "how do I get from here to there?" using the cheapest available route.

- Given transition cost annotations, compute weighted shortest path between any two states (Dijkstra or A*)
- Prefer routes that use deterministic transitions over vision-required ones, even if more hops
- Support route constraints: avoid specific states, prefer deterministic-only paths
- Eliminate backtracking to home screen: if you're deep in one branch and the next task starts in an adjacent branch, the graph knows the shortcut
- Support route recalculation when a transition fails (fallback paths)

## Phase 7: Ongoing Maintenance

Keep the graph accurate as the external system changes.

- Detect graph drift: observations that don't match any known state in the graph
- Flag unknown states for human review and graph updates
- Graceful degradation: fall back to vision path for unrecognized states rather than guessing
- Track anchor reliability over time: if an anchor starts failing intermittently, flag it before it causes consistent misclassification
- Support adding new states and transitions discovered during normal operation
- The graph improves passively with every session (validating existing states, discovering new patterns) and actively through dedicated maintenance passes

---

## Cross-Cutting Concerns (Not Phases)

These are not sequential phases; they apply throughout:

**Confidence thresholds and escalation.** Some states can tolerate low confidence ("80% sure, proceed"). Others (purchase confirmation, irreversible actions) require explicit vision/LLM review before the agent acts, even if the deterministic classifier is confident. When confidence is below threshold: escalate to vision. When vision is also uncertain: escalate to human. When human is unavailable: pause.

**Session management.** Maintain a running record of confirmed states and transitions for the current session. This is integral to `locate()` (constraining candidates) and to recovery after crashes. Session history is a signal, not just a log.

**Mock harness.** As soon as labeled screenshots and a graph definition exist, offline development becomes possible. Run anchors against captured screenshots without the live system. Iterate on the graph definition, test the classifier, refine anchors, all without consuming LLM tokens or needing the external system running. This is the development scaffold that enables fast iteration on everything else.

**Cross-session progress tracking.** Building a state graph is a multi-session effort. A structured PROGRESS.md file tracks: what's been explored, what's been consolidated, what decisions are pending, what issues are known, what the next session should focus on. Committed to the repo. Readable by any agent or human.

**Human guidance.** The human provides domain knowledge and judgment at specific decision points (state consolidation ambiguity, confirming irreversible actions, answering meta-questions about the system). The methodology makes the human's role clear: what questions will come, when to expect them, what to do while the agent works.

## codex: You are a file-organization agent working in C:\Us...

You are a file-organization agent working in C:\Users\pmacl\Downloads.

  Your job is to finalize organization from an existing messy Downloads tree. Do not move toward new folder depth changes. No flattening, no deepening, no deletions.

  Hard rules:
  1) NO DELETIONS.
  2) NO FLATTENING (do not collapse folder levels).
  3) NO DEEPENING (do not create new nested category folders).
  4) Do NOT edit file contents.
  5) Use file content to classify ambiguous names before renaming.
  6) Keep project internals coherent; do not touch code-structure semantics of existing repos.
  7) Provide a full, explicit log of every action.

  Primary objective:
  - Reorganize by name/path only for clarity by renaming only files/directories that are clearly ambiguous or conflicting.
  - Preserve current folder structure unless a move is required solely to fix impossible naming/ambiguity.
  - If a file is unclear, leave it where it is and flag it.

  Initial structure should remain:
  - 01_Projects, 02_Research, 03_Legal, 04_Presentations, 05_Media, 06_Installers, 07_Archives, 08_Exports, 09_Reference, 09_Resources, plus existing miscellaneous folders if present.
  - You may place unresolved items in 99_Misc only if that was already part of your baseline convention.

  Scope:
  - If file/folder names are generic (README, LICENSE, notes, temp, index, agent, unknown hash-like, short tokens, duplicated names), inspect contents to infer true purpose.
  - Rename with disambiguating context (examples: state-cartographer-README.md, plugin-creator-assets-README.md, policyholder-behavior-v2.md).
  - Never overwrite existing files. Resolve duplicates via suffixes/prefixing with source context.
  - Do not “guess” project membership; use content and nearby context.
  - Keep media files together by semantic type already present (e.g., under 05_Media), but do not move just for flattening.

  Required deliverables:
  1) `changes.json` (or markdown table) with columns:
     original_path | new_path | action | action_type (moved/renamed/left) | rationale | confidence
  2) List of ambiguous/untouched files (left untouched with reason).
  3) Rename map grouped by:
     - duplicate generic names,
     - content-generic names,
     - uncertain classifications.
  4) Summary metrics:
     - total files scanned
     - renamed files count
     - moved files count (if any)
     - unchanged files count
     - media file counts by extension
  5) Any empty directories left behind and whether left intentionally.

  Behavioral constraints:
  - Use sub-agents for ambiguous file review.
  - Do not perform extra edits after finishing unless asked.
  - Do not run tests or validation commands unless explicitly requested.
